These newly independent countries confronted spacefaring countries, demanding an anti-colonial stance and regulation of space activity when [space law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_law)was raised and negotiated internationally. Fears of confrontations because of [land grabs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_grab) and an [arms race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_race) [in space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Militarization_of_space) between the few countries with spaceflight capabilities grew and were ultimately shared by the spacefaring countries themselves.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Wilson_Center_2021-2) This produced the wording of the agreed on international space law, starting with the [Outer Space Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty) of 1967, calling space a "[province of all mankind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_heritage_of_humanity)" and securing provisions for international regulation and sharing of outer space.

> 
> 
> 
> A picture of the completed [Lunar Flag Assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Flag_Assembly). The USSR and the US engaged in dropping pennants[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Capelotti_2014_p._44-20) and raising flags on the Moon while not laying any claims over it, in accordance with the Outer Space Treaty.
> 

The advent of [geostationary satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geostationary_satellite) raised the case of limited space in outer space. A group of [equatorial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equator) countries, all of which were countries that were once colonies of colonial empires, but without spaceflight capabilities, signed in 1976 the [Bogota Declaration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bogota_Declaration). These countries declared that [geostationary orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geostationary_orbit) is a limited natural resource and belongs to the equatorial countries directly below, seeing it not as part of outer space, humanity's [common](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commons). Through this, the declaration challenged the dominance of geostationary orbit by spacefaring countries through identifying their dominance as imperialistic. Furthermore this dominance in space has foreshadowed threats to the Outer Space Treaty guaranteed accessibility to space, as in the case of [space debris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_debris) which is ever increasing because of a lack of access regulation.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Columbia_Journal_of_Transnational_Law_2017-21)[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Biondi_2018-22)[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Collis_2009_pp._47%E2%80%9365-1)

In 1977, the first sustained space habitat, the [Salyut 6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salyut_6) station, was put into Earth's orbit. Eventually the first space stations were succeeded by the [ISS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISS), today's largest [human outpost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_outpost) in space and closest to a space settlement. Built and operated under a multilateral regime, it has become a blueprint for future stations, such as [around](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway) and possibly [on the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moonbase).[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Foust_2018-23)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-ESA_Blog_Navigator_%E2%80%93_Navigator_page_for_active_ESA_blogs_2016-24) An international regime for lunar activity was demanded by the international [Moon Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_Treaty), but is currently developed multilaterally as with the [Artemis Accords](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_Accords).[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-The_Space_Review_2020-25)[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-The_Space_Treaty_Institute_%E2%80%93_Dedicated_to_Peace_and_Sustainability_in_Outer_Space._Our_Mission-26) The only habitation on a different celestial body so far have been the temporary habitats of the crewed [lunar landers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_lander). Similar to the Artemis program, China is leading an effort to develop a lunar base called the [International Lunar Research Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Lunar_Research_Station) beginning in the 2030s.

### **Conceptual**

*Further information: [Space colonization § In media and fiction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#In_media_and_fiction)*

Early suggestions for future colonists like [Francis Drake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Drake) and [Christopher Columbus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Columbus)to reach the Moon and people consequently living there were made by [John Wilkins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Wilkins) in *A Discourse Concerning a New Planet* in the first half of the 17th century.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-27)

The first known work on space colonization was the 1869 novella [*The Brick Moon*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Brick_Moon)by [Edward Everett Hale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Everett_Hale), about an inhabited artificial satellite.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-28) In 1897 [Kurd Lasswitz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurd_Lasswitz) also wrote about space colonies.

The Russian rocket science pioneer [Konstantin Tsiolkovsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky) foresaw elements of the space community in his book *Beyond Planet Earth* written about 1900. Tsiolkovsky had his space travelers building greenhouses and raising crops in space.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-29) Tsiolkovsky believed that going into space would help perfect human beings, leading to immortality and peace.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-bio-30)

One of the first to speak about space colonization was [Cecil Rhodes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes) who in 1902 spoke about "these stars that you see overhead at night, these vast worlds which we can never reach", adding "I would annex the planets if I could; I often think of that. It makes me sad to see them so clear and yet so far".[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-31)

In the 1920s [John Desmond Bernal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Desmond_Bernal), [Hermann Oberth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_Oberth), [Guido von Pirquet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guido_von_Pirquet) and [Herman Noordung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herman_Noordung) further developed the idea. [Wernher von Braun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun) contributed his ideas in a 1952 *Colliers* article. In the 1950s and 1960s, [Dandridge M. Cole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dandridge_M._Cole)[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-32)published his ideas.

Another seminal book on the subject was the book *The High Frontier: Human Colonies in Space* by [Gerard K. O'Neill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerard_K._O%27Neill)[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-:1-33) in 1977 which was followed the same year by *Colonies in Space* by [T. A. Heppenheimer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T._A._Heppenheimer).[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-34)

[Marianne J. Dyson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marianne_J._Dyson) wrote *Home on the Moon; Living on a Space Frontier* in 2003;[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-35) [Peter Eckart](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Peter_Eckart&action=edit&redlink=1) wrote *Lunar Base Handbook* in 2006[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-36) and then [Harrison Schmitt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harrison_Schmitt)'s *Return to the Moon* written in 2007.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-37)

## Locations

> 
> 
> 
> Artist Les Bossinas' 1989 concept of [Mars mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars)
> 

Location is a frequent point of contention between space colonization advocates. The location of colonization can be on a physical body [planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet), [dwarf planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_planet), [natural satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_satellite), or [asteroid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid) or orbiting one. Colonization of the Solar System has received the most attention.

For settlements not on a body see also [space habitat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_habitat).

### **Near-Earth space**

> 
> 
> 
> Artist's 1986 conception of a lunar base
> 

### **The Moon**

*Main article: [Colonization of the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Moon)*

The [Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon) is discussed as a target for colonization, due to its proximity to Earth and lower [escape velocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escape_velocity). Abundant [ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice) is trapped in [permanently shadowed craters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permanently_shadowed_crater) near the poles, which could provide support for the water needs of a lunar colony,[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-38) though indications that [mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(element)) is also similarly trapped there may pose health concerns.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-39)[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-prospecting-40) Native [precious metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precious_metal), such as [gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold), [silver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silver), and probably [platinum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platinum), are also concentrated at the lunar poles by electrostatic dust transport.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-prospecting-40) However, the Moon's lack of atmosphere provides no protection from space radiation or meteoroids, so [lunar lava tubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_lava_tube) have been proposed sites to gain protection.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-41) The Moon's low surface gravity is also a concern, as it is unknown whether 1/6[g](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-force) is enough to maintain human health for long periods.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-42)Interest in establishing a [moonbase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moonbase) has increased in the 21st century as an intermediate to Mars colonization, with such proposals as the [Moon Village](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_Village) for research, mining, and trade facilities with permanent habitation.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-43)

A number of government space agencies such as [Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Federal_Space_Agency) (2014),[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-44) [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration) (2012)[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-45)[[*needs update*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items)] and the [US](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA) (2012)[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-46) have periodically floated lunar plans for constructing the first lunar outpost.

The [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency) (ESA) head [Jan Woerner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann-Dietrich_W%C3%B6rner) at the International Astronautical Congress in Bremen, Germany, in October, 2018 proposed cooperation among countries and companies on lunar capabilities, a concept referred to as [*Moon Village*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moonbase#Moon_Village).[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-sn20180529-47)

In a December 2017 [directive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Policy_Directive_1), the [Trump Administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trump_Administration) steered [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA) to include a lunar mission on the pathway to other [beyond Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beyond_Earth_orbit) (BEO) destinations.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-spo20171217-48)[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-sn20180529-47)

In a May 2018 interview, [Blue Origin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Origin) CEO [Jeff Bezos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeff_Bezos) indicated Blue Origin would build and [fly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Glenn) the [*Blue Moon lunar lander*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Moon_(lander)) on its own, with [private funding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Private_spaceflight), but that they would build it a lot faster, and accomplish more, if it were done in a partnership with existing government space agencies. Bezos specifically mentioned the December 2017 [NASA direction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Policy_Directive_1) and the ESA *Moon Village* concepts.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-sn20180529-47)

In 2023, U.S. Defense Department started a [study](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA_lunar_programs) about the necessary infrastructure and capabilities required to develop a moon-based economy over the next ten years.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-49)

### **Lagrange points**

*Main article: [Lagrange point colonization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_point_colonization)*

> 
> 
> 
> A contour plot of the [gravitational potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_potential) of the Moon and Earth, showing the five Earth–Moon Lagrange points
> 

Another near-Earth possibility are the stable Earth–Moon [Lagrange points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrangian_point) [L4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_point#L4_and_L5) and [L5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_point#L4_and_L5), at which point a space colony can float indefinitely. The [L5 Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L5_Society) was founded to promote settlement by building space stations at these points. [Gerard K. O'Neill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerard_K._O%27Neill) suggested in 1974 that the L5 point, in particular, could fit several thousands of floating colonies, and would allow easy travel to and from the colonies due to the shallow [effective potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effective_potential) at this point.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-o'neill-50)

### **The inner planets**

Many planets within the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System) have been considered for colonization and [terraforming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terraforming). The main candidates for colonization in the inner Solar System are [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_Mars)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-51) and [Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_Venus).[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-52) Other possible candidates for colonization include the [Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Moon)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-53) and even [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_Mercury).[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-54)

### **Mercury**

> 
> 
> 
> An artist's conception of the terraformed [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet))
> 

Once thought to be a volatile-depleted body like the Moon, Mercury is now known to be volatile-rich, surprisingly richer in volatiles than any other terrestrial body in the inner Solar System.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-55) The planet also receives six and a half times the solar flux as the Earth/Moon system,[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Bolonkin-56) making solar energy a very effective energy source; it could be harnessed through orbital solar arrays and beamed to the surface or exported to other planets.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTMercury-57)

Geologist Stephen Gillett suggested in 1996 that this could make Mercury an ideal place to build and launch [solar sail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_sail) spacecraft, which could launch as folded-up "chunks" by [mass driver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_driver) from Mercury's surface. Once in space, the solar sails would deploy. Solar energy for the mass driver should be easy to come by, and solar sails near Mercury would have 6.5 times the thrust they do near Earth. This could make Mercury an ideal place to acquire materials useful in building hardware to send to (and terraform) Venus. Vast solar collectors could also be built on or near Mercury to produce power for large-scale engineering activities such as laser-pushed light sails to nearby star systems.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-58)

As Mercury has essentially no axial tilt, crater floors near its poles lie in [eternal darkness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crater_of_eternal_darkness), never seeing the Sun. They function as [cold traps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_trap_(astronomy)), trapping volatiles for geological periods. It is estimated that the poles of Mercury contain 1014–1015 kg of water, likely covered by about 5.65×109 m3 of hydrocarbons. This would make agriculture possible. It has been suggested that plant varieties could be developed to take advantage of the high light intensity and the long day of Mercury. The poles do not experience the significant day-night variations the rest of Mercury do, making them the best place on the planet to begin a colony.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Bolonkin-56)

Another option is to live underground, where day-night variations would be damped enough that temperatures would stay roughly constant. There are indications that Mercury contains [lava tubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lava_tube), like the Moon and Mars, which would be suitable for this purpose.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTMercury-57) Underground temperatures in a ring around Mercury's poles can even reach room temperature on Earth, 22±1 °C; and this is achieved at a depths starting from only about 0.7 m. This presence of volatiles and abundance of energy has led [Alexander Bolonkin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Bolonkin) and James Shifflett to consider Mercury preferable to Mars for colonization.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Bolonkin-56)[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-59)

Yet a third option could be to continually move to stay on the night side, as Mercury's 176-day-long day-night cycle means that the [terminator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terminator_(solar)) travels very slowly.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTMercury-57)

Because Mercury is very dense, its surface gravity is 0.38g like Mars, even though it is a smaller planet.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Bolonkin-56) This would be easier to adjust to than lunar gravity (0.16g), but still present advantages regarding lower escape velocity from the planet.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTMercury-57)Mercury's proximity gives it advantages over the asteroids and outer planets, and its low [synodic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synodic_period) means that launch windows from Earth to Mercury are more frequent than those from Earth to Venus or Mars.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTMercury-57)

On the downside, a Mercury colony would require significant shielding from radiation and solar flares, and since Mercury is airless, decompression and temperature extremes would be constant risks.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTMercury-57)

### **Venus**

*Main article: [Colonization of Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_Venus)*

> 
> 
> 
> An artist's conception of a [research station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Research_station) in the clouds of [Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus)
> 

Surface conditions on Venus are extremely hostile to human life: average surface temperature is 464 °C (hot enough to melt lead), and average surface pressure is 92 times Earth's atmospheric pressure – roughly equivalent to a depth of one kilometre under Earth's oceans.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-fact-60) (There is some variation; due to its altitude, the peak of [Maxwell Montes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxwell_Montes)is at only 380 °C and 45 bar, making it the coolest and least pressurised location on Venus' surface.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Basilevsky_2003-61)[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-McGill_2010-62) There are also some hot spots at about 700 °C.) Solar energy is not available at the surface due to the constant cloud cover, and the carbon dioxide atmosphere is poisonous.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-landis1-63)

However, the upper atmosphere of Venus has much more Earthlike conditions and has been suggested as a plausible colonization location since at least 1971 by Soviet scientists.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Badescu-64) At just over 50 km altitude (the cloud tops), atmospheric pressure is roughly equal to that on Earth's surface, and temperatures range from 0–50 °C. The volatile elements necessary for life are present (hydrogen, carbon, nitrogen, oxygen, and sulfur), and above the clouds, solar energy is abundant. Pressurization would not be required; humans could even go outside the habitats safely with oxygen provision and clothing to protect against the sulfuric acid droplets. [Geoffrey Landis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Landis) has pointed out that breathable air is a lifting gas in Venus' atmosphere: a cubic meter of air would lift half a kilogram, and an oxygen- and nitrogen-filled aerostat the size of a city on Venus would be able to lift the mass of a city. This suggests floating aerostat cities as a colonization method for Venus. The lack of pressure differences between the outside and inside means that there is ample time to repair habitat breaches. With just over three times the land area of Earth, there would be space even for a billion such cities.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-landis1-63) The atmosphere provides enough radiation shielding at this altitude, and Venus' 0.90g gravity is likely sufficient to prevent the negative health effects of microgravity.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-landis1-63)

A day on Venus is very long on the surface, but the atmosphere rotates much faster than the planet (a phenomenon called [superrotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_super-rotation)), so a floating habitat would only have a day of about a hundred hours. Landis compares this favorably with [polar days](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_day) and nights on Earth, which are much longer. A floating habitat at higher latitudes on Venus would approach a normal 24-hour cycle. Mining the surface would give access to important industrial metals, and it could be accessed via airplanes, balloons, or fullerene cables meant to withstand high temperatures. To avoid the problem of the habitat being in motion relative to its mining devices, the habitat could descend into the lower atmosphere: this region is hotter, but Landis argues that a large-sized habitat would have enough [heat capacity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_capacity) to have no problem with a short stay at higher temperatures.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-landis1-63)

The colonization of Venus has been a subject of many works of [science fiction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_fiction)since before the dawn of [spaceflight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaceflight) and is still discussed from both a fictional and a scientific standpoint. Proposals for Venus are focused on colonies floating in the upper-middle atmosphere[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-65) and on [terraforming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terraforming_of_Venus).

### **Mars**

*Main article: [Colonization of Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_Mars)*

> 
> 
> 
> An artist's conception of a [human mission to Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars)
> 

The hypothetical colonization of Mars has received interest from public space agencies and private corporations and has received extensive treatment in science fiction writing, film, and art. The most recent[[*when?*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items)]commitments to researching permanent settlement include those by public space agencies—[NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA), [ESA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency), [Roscosmos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roscosmos), [ISRO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation), and the [CNSA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration)—and private organizations—[SpaceX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX), [Lockheed Martin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lockheed_Martin), and [Boeing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing).[[*citation needed*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed)]

### **Asteroid belt**

*Main article: [Colonization of the asteroid belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_the_asteroid_belt)*

*See also: [Asteroids § Exploration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroids#Exploration)*

The [asteroid belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_belt) has about 1018 [metric tonnes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_tonne) of overall material available – ten thousand times more than is available in the [near-Earth asteroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroids)[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-taylor-66) – but it is thinly distributed as it covers a vast region of space. The largest asteroid is [Ceres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)), which at about 940 km in diameter is big enough to be a [dwarf planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_planet). The next two largest are [Pallas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2_Pallas) and [Vesta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4_Vesta), both about 520 km in diameter. Uncrewed supply craft should be practical with little technological advance, even crossing 500 million kilometers of space. The colonists would have a strong interest in assuring their asteroid did not hit Earth or any other body of significant mass, but would have extreme difficulty in moving an asteroid[[*citation needed*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed)] of any size. The orbits of the Earth and most asteroids are very distant from each other in terms of delta-v and the asteroidal bodies have enormous [momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Momentum). Rockets or mass drivers can perhaps be installed on asteroids to direct their path into a safe course.

Ceres has readily available water, ammonia, and methane, important for survival, fuel, and possibly terraforming of Mars and Venus. The colony could be established on a surface crater or underground.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTCeres-67) However, even Ceres only manages a tiny surface gravity of 0.03g, which is not enough to stave off the negative effects of microgravity (though it does make transportation to and from Ceres easier). Either medical treatments or artificial gravity would thus be required. Additionally, colonizing the main asteroid belt would likely require infrastructure to already be present on the Moon and Mars.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTCeres-67)

Some have suggested that Ceres could act as a main base or hub for asteroid mining.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTCeres-67) However, [Geoffrey A. Landis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_A._Landis) has pointed out that the asteroid belt is a poor place for an asteroid-mining base if more than one asteroid is to be exploited: the asteroids are not close to each other, and two asteroids chosen at random are quite likely to be on opposite sides from the Sun from each other. He suggests that it would be better to construct such a base on an inner planet, such as Venus: inner planets have higher orbital velocities, making the transfer time to any specific asteroid shorter, and orbit the Sun faster, so that the launch windows to the asteroid are more frequent (a lower [synodic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synodic_period)). Thus Venus is closer to the asteroids than Earth or Mars in terms of flight time. Transfer times for the journeys Venus–Ceres and Venus–Vesta are only 1.15 and 0.95 years respectively along minimum-energy trajectories, which is shorter even than Earth–Ceres and Earth–Vesta at 1.29 and 1.08 years respectively.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-landis1-63) Anthony Taylor, Jonathan C. McDowell, and Martin Elvis suggest Mars' moon [Phobos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phobos_(moon)) as an asteroid-belt mining hub: the main belt is much more accessible from Martian orbit than from low Earth orbit in terms of [delta-v](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delta-v), the moon provides a large platform and a mass for radiation shielding, and it is not far from Mars' surface. Hence, a Phobos base for asteroid mining works hand in hand economically with Mars settlement.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-taylor-66)

### **Moons of outer planets**

> 
> 
> 
> Artist's impression of a hypothetical ocean [cryobot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cryobot) in [Europa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europa_(moon))
> 

Human missions to the outer planets would need to arrive quickly due to the effects of space radiation and microgravity along the journey.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Palaszewski-68) In 2012, Thomas B. Kerwick wrote that the distance to the outer planets made their human exploration impractical for now, noting that travel times for round trips to Mars were estimated at two years, and that the closest approach of Jupiter to Earth is over ten times farther than the closest approach of Mars to Earth. However, he noted that this could change with "significant advancement on spacecraft design".[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69) [Nuclear-thermal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_thermal_rocket) or nuclear-electric engines have been suggested as a way to make the journey to Jupiter in a reasonable amount of time.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTJupiter-70) Another possibility would be plasma [magnet sails](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_sail), a technology already suggested for sending a probe to Jupiter rapidly.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-71) The cold would also be a factor, necessitating a robust source of heat energy for spacesuits and bases.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69) Most of the larger moons of the outer planets contain [water ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice), [liquid water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liquid_water), and organic compounds that might be useful for sustaining human life.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-icemoons-72)[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-liftveil-73)

[Robert Zubrin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Zubrin) has suggested Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune as advantageous locations for colonization because their atmospheres are good sources of fusion fuels, such as [deuterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deuterium) and [helium-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-3). Zubrin suggested that Saturn would be the most important and valuable as it is the closest and has an excellent satellite system. Jupiter's high gravity makes it difficult to extract gases from its atmosphere, and its strong radiation belt makes developing its system difficult.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTSaturn-74) On the other hand, fusion power has yet to be achieved, and fusion power from helium-3 is more difficult to achieve than conventional [deuterium–tritium fusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deuterium%E2%80%93tritium_fusion).[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-75) Jeffrey Van Cleve, Carl Grillmair, and Mark Hanna instead focus on Uranus, because the [delta-v](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delta-v)required to get helium-3 from the atmosphere into orbit is half that needed for Jupiter, and because Uranus' atmosphere is five times richer in helium than Saturn's.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-He3U-76)

Jupiter's [Galilean moons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galilean_moons) (Io, Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto) and Saturn's [Titan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titan_(moon))are the only moons that have gravities comparable to Earth's Moon. The Moon has a 0.17g gravity; Io, 0.18g; Europa, 0.13g; Ganymede, 0.15g; Callisto, 0.13g; and Titan, 0.14g. Neptune's [Triton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triton_(moon)) has about half the Moon's gravity (0.08g); other [round moons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_moon) provide even less (starting from Uranus' [Titania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titania_(moon)) and [Oberon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oberon_(moon)) at about 0.04g).[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69)

### **Jovian moons**

> 
> 
> 
> Artist's impression of a base on Callisto[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-CallistoBase-77)
> 

| Moon | https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sievert/day |
| --- | --- |
| Io | 36https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-78 |
| Europa | 5.40https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-ringwald-79 |
| Ganymede | 0.08https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-ringwald-79 |
| Callisto | 0.0001https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-ringwald-79 |

The [Jovian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter) system in general has particular disadvantages for colonization, including a deep [gravity well](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity_well). The [magnetosphere of Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Jupiter)bombards the [moons of Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter) with intense [ionizing radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionizing_radiation)[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-radjup-80) delivering about 36 [Sv](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sievert#Dose_examples) per day to unshielded colonists on [Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon)) and about 5.40 Sv per day on [Europa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europa_(moon)). Exposure to about 0.75 Sv over a few days is enough to cause [radiation poisoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome), and about 5 Sv over a few days is fatal.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-zoob2jvn-81)

Jupiter itself, like the other gas giants, has further disadvantages. There is no accessible surface on which to land, and the light hydrogen atmosphere would not provide good buoyancy for some kind of aerial habitat as has been proposed for Venus.

Radiation levels on [Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon)) and [Europa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europa_(moon)) are extreme, enough to kill unshielded humans within an Earth day.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-zoob2ttn-82) Therefore, only [Callisto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Callisto_(moon)) and perhaps [Ganymede](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)) could reasonably support a human colony. Callisto orbits outside Jupiter's radiation belt.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69) Ganymede's low latitudes are partially shielded by the moon's magnetic field, though not enough to completely remove the need for radiation shielding. Both of them have available water, silicate rock, and metals that could be mined and used for construction.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69)

Although Io's volcanism and tidal heating constitute valuable resources, exploiting them is probably impractical.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69) Europa is rich in water (its subsurface ocean is expected to contain over twice as much water as all Earth's oceans together)[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTJupiter-70)and likely oxygen, but metals and minerals would have to be imported. If alien microbial life exists on Europa, human immune systems may not protect against it. Sufficient radiation shielding might, however, make Europa an interesting location for a research base.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69) The private [*Artemis Project*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_Project) drafted a plan in 1997 to colonize Europa, involving surface igloos as bases to drill down into the ice and explore the ocean underneath, and suggesting that humans could live in "air pockets" in the ice layer.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-83)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-84)[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTJupiter-70) Ganymede[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTJupiter-70) and Callisto are also expected to have internal oceans.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-OW_Roadmap_2019-85) It might be possible to build a surface base that would produce fuel for further exploration of the Solar System.

In 2003, NASA performed a study called *HOPE* (Revolutionary Concepts for Human Outer Planet Exploration) regarding the future exploration of the Solar System.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-86) The target chosen was [Callisto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Callisto_(moon)) due to its distance from Jupiter, and thus the planet's harmful radiation. It could be possible to build a surface base that would produce fuel for further exploration of the Solar System. HOPE estimated a round trip time for a crewed mission of about 2–5 years, assuming significant progress in propulsion technologies.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Kerwick-69)

[Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon)) is not ideal for colonization, due to its hostile environment. The moon is under influence of high tidal forces, causing high volcanic activity. Jupiter's strong radiation belt overshadows Io, delivering 36 Sv a day to the moon. The moon is also extremely dry. Io is the least ideal place for the colonization of the four Galilean moons. Despite this, its volcanoes could be energy resources for the other moons, which are better suited to colonization.

> 
> 
> 
> The magnetic field of Jupiter and co-rotation rotation enforcing currents
> 

[Ganymede](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)) is the largest moon in the Solar System. Ganymede is the only moon with a [magnetosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere), albeit overshadowed by [Jupiter's magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Jupiter). Because of this magnetic field, Ganymede is one of only two Jovian moons where surface settlements would be feasible because it receives about 0.08 [Sv](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sievert) of radiation per day. Ganymede could be terraformed.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-ringwald-79)

The [Keck Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keck_Observatory) announced in 2006 that the binary [Jupiter trojan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter_trojan)[617 Patroclus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/617_Patroclus), and possibly many other Jupiter trojans, are likely composed of water ice, with a layer of dust. This suggests that mining water and other volatiles in this region and transporting them elsewhere in the Solar System, perhaps via the proposed [Interplanetary Transport Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interplanetary_Transport_Network), may be feasible in the not-so-distant future. This could make [colonization of the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Moon), [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_Mercury) and main-belt [asteroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_the_asteroids)more practical.

### **Saturnian moons**

*See also: [Colonization of Titan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_Titan)*

> 
> 
> 
> [Ligeia Mare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ligeia_Mare), a sea on [Titan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titan_(moon))(left) compared at scale to [Lake Superior](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Superior) on Earth (right)
> 

Saturn has seven moons [large enough to be round](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_moon): in order of increasing distance from Saturn, they are [Mimas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimas_(moon)), [Enceladus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enceladus), [Tethys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tethys_(moon)), [Dione](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dione_(moon)), [Rhea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhea_(moon)), [Titan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titan_(moon)), and [Iapetus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)). Titan is the largest and the only one with a Moon-like gravity: it is the only moon in the Solar System to have a dense atmosphere and is rich in carbon-bearing compounds, suggesting it as a colonization target.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-zoob2ttn-82) Titan has water ice and large methane oceans.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-NasaNews-87) [Robert Zubrin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Zubrin)identified Titan as possessing an abundance of all the elements necessary to support life, making Titan perhaps the most advantageous locale in the outer Solar System for colonization.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-zoob2ttn-82)

The small moon Enceladus is also of interest, having a subsurface ocean that is separated from the surface by only tens of meters of ice at the south pole, compared to kilometers of ice separating the ocean from the surface on Europa. Volatile and organic compounds are present there, and the moon's high density for an ice world (1.6 g/cm3) indicates that its core is rich in silicates.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTSaturn-74)

Saturn's radiation belt is much weaker than Jupiter's, so radiation is less of an issue here. Dione, Rhea, Titan, and Iapetus all orbit outside the radiation belt, and Titan's thick atmosphere would adequately shield against cosmic radiation.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-UTSaturn-74)

[Robert Zubrin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Zubrin) identified [Saturn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saturn), [Uranus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranus) and [Neptune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune) as "the [Persian Gulf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_Gulf) of the Solar System", as the largest sources of [deuterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deuterium) and helium-3 to drive a [fusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_fusion)economy, with Saturn the most important and most valuable of the three, because of its relative proximity, low radiation, and large system of moons.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-zoob2pgs-88) On the other hand, planetary scientist [John Lewis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_S._Lewis) in his 1997 book [*Mining the Sky*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mining_the_Sky), insists that Uranus is the likeliest place to mine helium-3 because of its significantly shallower gravity well, which makes it easier for a laden tanker spacecraft to thrust itself out. Furthermore, Uranus is an [ice giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_giant), which would likely make it easier to separate the helium out of the atmosphere.

Zubrin identified [Titan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titan_(moon)) as possessing an abundance of all the elements necessary to support life, making Titan perhaps the most advantageous locale in the outer Solar System for colonization. He said, "In certain ways, Titan is the most hospitable extraterrestrial world within the Solar System for human colonization."[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-zoob2ttn-82)A widely published expert on [terraforming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terraforming), [Christopher McKay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_McKay_(planetary_scientist)), is also a co-investigator on the [*Huygens*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huygens_probe) [probe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huygens_probe) that landed on Titan in January 2005.

The surface of Titan is mostly uncratered and thus inferred to be very young and active, and probably composed of mostly water ice, and lakes of liquid hydrocarbons (methane/ethane) in its polar regions. While the temperature is cryogenic (95 K) it should be able to support a base, but more information regarding Titan's surface and the activities on it is necessary. The thick atmosphere and the weather, such as potential flash floods, are also factors to consider.

On 9 March 2006, [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA)'s [*Cassini*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens) space probe found possible evidence of liquid water on [Enceladus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enceladus).[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-89) According to that article, "pockets of liquid water may be no more than tens of meters below the surface." These findings were confirmed in 2014 by NASA. This means liquid water could be collected much more easily and safely on Enceladus than, for instance, on Europa (see above). Discovery of water, especially liquid water, generally makes a celestial body a much more likely candidate for colonization. An alternative model of Enceladus's activity is the decomposition of methane/water [clathrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clathrate) – a process requiring lower temperatures than liquid water eruptions. The higher density of Enceladus indicates a larger than Saturnian average silicate core that could provide materials for base operations.

### **Trans-Neptunian region**

*Main article: [Colonization of trans-Neptunian objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_trans-Neptunian_objects)*

[Freeman Dyson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freeman_Dyson) suggested that within a few centuries human civilization will have relocated to the [Kuiper belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuiper_belt).[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-90)[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-91) Several hundred billion to trillion comet-like ice-rich bodies exist outside the orbit of Neptune, in the Kuiper belt and Inner and Outer Oort cloud. These may contain all the ingredients for life (water ice, ammonia, and carbon-rich compounds), including significant amounts of deuterium and helium-3. Since Dyson's proposal, the number of trans-Neptunian objects known has increased greatly.

Colonists could live in the dwarf planet's icy crust or mantle, using fusion or geothermal heat[[*citation needed*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed)] and mining the soft-ice or liquid inner ocean for volatiles and minerals. Given the light gravity and resulting lower pressure in the ice mantle or inner ocean, colonizing the rocky core's outer surface might give colonists the largest number of mineral and volatile resources as well as insulating them from cold.[[*citation needed*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed)] Surface habitats or domes are another possibility, as background radiation levels are likely to be low.[[*citation needed*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed)]

### **Orbit around giant planets**

There have also been proposals to place robotic [aerostats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerostat) in the upper atmospheres of the Solar System's [giant planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giant_planet) for exploration and possibly mining of [helium-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-3), which could have a very high value per unit mass as a thermonuclear fuel.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-zoob2sop-92)[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-He3U-76)

Because [Uranus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranus) has the lowest [escape velocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escape_velocity) of the four giant planets, it has been proposed as a mining site for [helium-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-3).[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-He3U-76) If human supervision of the robotic activity proved necessary, one of [Uranus's natural satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranus%27s_natural_satellites) might serve as a base.[[*according to whom?*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions)]

It is hypothesized that one of [Neptune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune)'s satellites could be used for colonization. [Triton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triton_(moon))'s surface shows signs of extensive geological activity that implies a subsurface ocean, perhaps composed of ammonia/water.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-93) If technology advanced to the point that tapping such geothermal energy was possible, it could make colonizing a cryogenic world like Triton feasible, supplemented by [nuclear fusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_fusion) power.[[*citation needed*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed)]

### **Beyond the Solar System**

## Law, governance, and sovereignty

*Main articles: [Space law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_law), [Space policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_policy), [Common heritage of humanity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_heritage_of_humanity), and [Extraterrestrial real estate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extraterrestrial_real_estate)*

Space activity is legally based on the [Outer Space Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty), the main international treaty. But [space law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_law) has become a larger legal field, which includes other international agreements such as the significantly less ratified [Moon Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_Treaty) and diverse national laws.

The Outer Space Treaty established the basic ramifications for space activity in article one: "The exploration and use of outer space, including the Moon and other celestial bodies, shall be carried out for the benefit and in the interests of all countries, irrespective of their degree of economic or scientific development, and shall be the province of all mankind."

And continued in article two by stating: "Outer space, including the Moon and other celestial bodies, is not subject to national appropriation by claim of sovereignty, by means of use or occupation, or by any other means."[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-unoda-97)

The development of international space law has revolved much around outer space being defined as [common heritage of mankind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_heritage_of_mankind). The *Magna Carta of Space*presented by William A. Hyman in 1966 framed outer space explicitly not as [*terra nullius*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terra_nullius) but as [*res communis*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Res_communis), which subsequently influenced the work of the [United Nations Committee on the Peaceful Uses of Outer Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Committee_on_the_Peaceful_Uses_of_Outer_Space).[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Durrani_2019-98)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-99)

## Reasons

### **Survival of human civilization**

*Main article: [Space and survival](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_and_survival)*

A primary argument calling for space colonization is the long-term survival of human civilization and terrestrial life.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-101) By developing alternative locations off Earth, the planet's species, including humans, could live on in the event of [natural or human-made disasters on our own planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risk).[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-102)

On two occasions, theoretical physicist and cosmologist [Stephen Hawking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Hawking) argued for space colonization as a means of saving humanity. In 2001, Hawking predicted that the human race would become extinct within the next thousand years unless colonies could be established in space.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-103) In 2010, he stated that humanity faces two options: either we colonize space within the next two hundred years, or we will face the long-term prospect of [extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_extinction).[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-104)

In 2005, then [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA) Administrator [Michael Griffin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_D._Griffin) identified space colonization as the ultimate goal of current spaceflight programs, saying:

... the goal isn't just scientific exploration ... it's also about extending the range of human habitat out from Earth into the solar system as we go forward in time ... In the long run, a single-planet species will not survive ... If we humans want to survive for hundreds of thousands of millions of years, we must ultimately populate other planets. Now, today the technology is such that this is barely conceivable. We're in the infancy of it. ... I'm talking about that one day, I don't know when that day is, but there will be more human beings who live off the Earth than on it. We may well have people living on the Moon. We may have people living on the moons of Jupiter and other planets. We may have people making habitats on asteroids ... I know that humans will colonize the solar system and one day go beyond.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-105)

[Louis J. Halle](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Louis_J._Halle&action=edit&redlink=1), formerly of the [United States Department of State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State), wrote in [*Foreign Affairs*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_Affairs) (Summer 1980) that the colonization of space will protect humanity in the event of global [nuclear warfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_warfare).[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-106) The physicist [Paul Davies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Davies) also supports the view that if a planetary catastrophe threatens the survival of the human species on Earth, a self-sufficient colony could "reverse-colonize" Earth and restore [human civilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civilization). The author and journalist [William E. Burrows](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_E._Burrows) and the biochemist [Robert Shapiro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Shapiro_(chemist)) proposed a private project, the [Alliance to Rescue Civilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alliance_to_Rescue_Civilization), with the goal of establishing an off-Earth "[backup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Backup)" of human civilization.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-107)

Based on his [Copernican principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copernican_principle), [J. Richard Gott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._Richard_Gott) has estimated that the human race could survive for another 7.8 million years, but it is not likely to ever colonize other planets. However, he expressed a hope to be proven wrong, because "colonizing other worlds is our best chance to hedge our bets and improve the survival prospects of our species".[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-108)

In a theoretical study from 2019, a group of researchers have pondered the long-term trajectory of human civilization.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-sb01-109) It is argued that due to Earth's finitude as well as the [limited duration of the Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System#The_Sun_and_planetary_environments), mankind's survival into the far future will very likely require extensive space colonization.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-sb01-109): 8, 22f  This 'astronomical trajectory' of mankind, as it is termed, could come about in four steps: First step, plenty of space colonies could be established at various habitable locations — be it in outer space or on [celestial bodies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_object) away from Earth – and allowed to remain dependent on support from Earth for a start. In the second step, these colonies could gradually become self-sufficient, enabling them to survive if or when the mother civilization on Earth fails or dies. Third step, the colonies could develop and expand their habitation by themselves on their [space stations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_station) or celestial bodies, for example via [terraforming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terraforming). In the fourth step, the colonies could self-replicate and establish new colonies further into space, a process that could then repeat itself and continue at an [exponential rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponential_growth) throughout the cosmos. However, this astronomical trajectory may not be a lasting one, as it will most likely be interrupted and eventually decline due to resource depletion or straining competition between various human factions, bringing about some 'star wars' scenario.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-sb01-109): 23–25

### **Vast resources in space**

*See also: [Steady-state economy § Pushing some of the terrestrial limits into outer space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steady-state_economy#Pushing_some_of_the_terrestrial_limits_into_outer_space)*

Resources in space, both in materials and energy, are enormous. The [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System) alone has, according to different estimates, enough material and energy to support anywhere from several thousand to over a billion times that of the current Earth-based human population, mostly from the Sun itself.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-110)[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-111)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-112)

Asteroid mining will also be a key player in space colonization. Water and materials to make structures and shielding can be easily found in asteroids. Instead of resupplying on Earth, mining and fuel stations need to be established on asteroids to facilitate better space travel.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-113) Optical mining is the term NASA uses to describe extracting materials from asteroids. NASA believes by using propellant derived from asteroids for exploration to the moon, Mars, and beyond will save $100 billion. If funding and technology come sooner than estimated, asteroid mining might be possible within a decade.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-114)

Although some items of the infrastructure requirements above can already be easily produced on Earth and would therefore not be very valuable as trade items (oxygen, water, base metal ores, silicates, etc.), other high-value items are more abundant, more easily produced, of higher quality, or can only be produced in space. These would provide (over the long-term) a very high return on the initial investment in space infrastructure.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-115)

Some of these high-value trade goods include precious metals,[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-members.nova.org-116)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-117) gemstones,[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-tricitiesnet.com-118) power,[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-119) solar cells,[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-panix.com-120) ball bearings,[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-panix.com-120) semi-conductors,[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-panix.com-120) and pharmaceuticals.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-panix.com-120)

The mining and extraction of metals from a small asteroid the size of [3554 Amun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/3554_Amun) or [(6178) 1986 DA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/(6178)_1986_DA), both small near-Earth asteroids, would be 30 times as much metal as humans have mined throughout history. A metal asteroid this size would be worth approximately US$20 trillion at 2001 market prices[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-121)

The main impediments to commercial exploitation of these resources are the very high cost of initial investment,[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-122) the very long period required for the expected return on those investments (*The Eros Project* plans a 50-year development),[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-123)and the fact that the venture has never been carried out before—the high-risk nature of the investment.

### **Expansion with fewer negative consequences**

*Further information: [Holocene extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holocene_extinction)*

Expansion of humans and technological progress has usually resulted in some form of environmental devastation, and destruction of [ecosystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystem) and their accompanying [wildlife](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildlife). In the past, expansion has often come at the expense of displacing many [indigenous peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_peoples), the resulting treatment of these peoples ranging anywhere from encroachment to genocide. Because space has no known life, this need not be a consequence, as some space settlement advocates have pointed out.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-124)[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-125) However, on some bodies of the Solar System, there is the potential for extant native lifeforms and so the negative consequences of space colonization cannot be dismissed.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-126)

Counterarguments state that changing only the location but not the logic of exploitation will not create a more sustainable future.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Yun-127)

### **Alleviating overpopulation and resource demand**

An argument for space colonization is to mitigate proposed impacts of [overpopulation of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_overpopulation), such as [resource depletion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Resource_depletion).[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-128) If the resources of space were opened to use and viable life-supporting habitats were built, Earth would no longer define the limitations of growth. Although many of Earth's resources are non-renewable, off-planet colonies could satisfy the majority of the planet's resource requirements. With the availability of extraterrestrial resources, demand on terrestrial ones would decline.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-129) Proponents of this idea include [Stephen Hawking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Hawking)[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-130) and [Gerard K. O'Neill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerard_K._O%27Neill).[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-:1-33)

Others including cosmologist [Carl Sagan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Sagan) and science fiction writers [Arthur C. Clarke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke),[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-clarkebipeds-131) and [Isaac Asimov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Asimov),[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-132) have argued that shipping any excess population into space is not a viable solution to human overpopulation. According to Clarke, "the population battle must be fought or won here on Earth".[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-clarkebipeds-131) The problem for these authors is not the lack of resources in space (as shown in books such as *Mining the Sky*[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-133)), but the physical impracticality of shipping vast numbers of people into space to "solve" overpopulation on Earth.

### **Other arguments**

Advocates for space colonization cite a presumed innate human drive to explore and discover, and call it a quality at the core of progress and thriving civilizations.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-134)[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-135)

[Nick Bostrom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bostrom) has argued that from a [utilitarian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utilitarianism) perspective, space colonization should be a chief goal as it would enable a very large population to live for a very long period of time (possibly billions of years), which would produce an enormous amount of utility (or happiness).[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-136) He claims that it is more important to reduce existential risks to increase the probability of eventual colonization than to accelerate technological development so that space colonization could happen sooner. In his paper, he assumes that the created lives will have positive ethical value despite the problem of [suffering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suffering).

In a 2001 interview with Freeman Dyson, J. Richard Gott and Sid Goldstein, they were asked for reasons why some humans should live in space.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-dyson-11) Their answers were:

- Spread life and beauty throughout the universe
- Ensure the survival of our species
- Make money through new forms of [space commercialization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercialization_of_space) such as [solar-power satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space-based_solar_power), [asteroid mining](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_mining), and [space manufacturing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_manufacturing)
- Save the [environment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)) of Earth by moving people and industry into space

[Biotic ethics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biotic_ethics) is a branch of ethics that values life itself. For biotic ethics, and their extension to space as panbiotic ethics, it is a human purpose to secure and propagate life and to use space to maximize life.

## Difficulties

[Untitled Database](%E2%AD%90%EF%B8%8F%20SpaceRAG%20Text%201c747df749744da19743ce68743431af/Untitled%20Database%20c46e8591813b42199410d03d6de8a80c.csv)

There would be many problems in colonizing the outer Solar System. These include:

- Distance from [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth) - The outer planets are much farther from Earth than the inner planets, and would therefore be harder and more time-consuming to reach. In addition, return voyages may well be prohibitive considering the time and distance.
- Extreme cold - temperatures are near [absolute zero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_zero) in many parts of the outer Solar System.
- Power - [Solar power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_power) is many times less concentrated in the outer Solar System than in the inner Solar System. It is unclear as to whether it would be usable there, using some form of concentration mirrors, or whether [nuclear power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_power)would be necessary. There have also been proposals to use the [gravitational potential energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_energy) of planets or dwarf planets with moons.
- [Effects of low gravity on the human body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body#Weightlessness) - All moons of the gas giants and all outer dwarf planets have a very low gravity, the highest being [Io's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon)) gravity (0.183 g) which is less than 1/5 of the Earth's gravity. Since the [Apollo program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_program) all crewed spaceflight has been constrained to [Low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit) and there has been no opportunity to test the effects of such low gravitational accelerations on the human body. It is speculated (but not confirmed) that the low gravity environments might have very similar effects to long-term exposure in [weightlessness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weightlessness). Such effects can be avoided by [rotating spacecraft creating artificial gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_gravity#Centripetal_force).
- Dust - breathing risks associated with fine dust from rocky surface objects, for similar reasons as [harmful effects of lunar dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_soil#Harmful_effects_of_lunar_dust).

## Criticisms

Space colonization has been seen as a relief to the problem of [human overpopulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_overpopulation) as early as 1758,[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-tsr2009-137) and listed as one of Stephen Hawking's reasons for pursuing space exploration.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-138) Critics note, however, that a slowdown in population growth rates since the 1980s has alleviated the risk of overpopulation.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-tsr2009-137)

Critics also argue that the costs of commercial activity in space are too high to be profitable against Earth-based industries, and hence that it is unlikely to see significant exploitation of space resources in the foreseeable future.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Marshall-139)

Other objections include concerns that the forthcoming colonization and [commodification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodification) of the cosmos is likely to enhance the interests of the already powerful, including major economic and military institutions e.g. the large financial institutions, the major aerospace companies and the [military–industrial complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military%E2%80%93industrial_complex), to lead to new [wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War), and to exacerbate pre-existing exploitation of [workers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploitation_of_labour) and [resources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources), [economic inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_inequality), [poverty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poverty), [social division](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_division) and [marginalization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_exclusion), environmental degradation, and other detrimental processes or institutions.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Dickens-MR-2010-11-7)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Dickens-MR-2008-02-8)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Dickens-MR-2017-03-9)

Additional concerns include creating a culture in which humans are no longer seen as human, but rather as material assets. The issues of [human dignity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_dignity), [morality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morality), [philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy), [culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture), [bioethics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bioethics), and the threat of megalomaniac leaders in these new "societies" would all have to be addressed in order for space colonization to meet the [psychological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Psychological) and [social](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_(sociology)) needs of people living in isolated colonies.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-140)

As an alternative or addendum for the future of the human race, many science fiction writers have focused on the realm of the 'inner-space', that is the computer-aided exploration of the [human mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mind) and human [consciousness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness)—possibly en route developmentally to a [Matrioshka Brain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matrioshka_Brain).[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-141)

[Robotic spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft) are proposed as an alternative to gain many of the same scientific advantages without the limited mission duration and high cost of life support and return transportation involved in human missions.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-142)

A corollary to the [Fermi paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi_paradox)—"nobody else is doing it"[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-143)—is the argument that, because no evidence of [alien colonization technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi_paradox#Alien_constructs)[[*broken anchor*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MOS:BROKENSECTIONLINKS)] exists, it is statistically unlikely to even be possible to use that same level of technology ourselves.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-144)

### **Colonialism**

*See also: [Manifest destiny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manifest_destiny), [Space advocacy § Decolonizing space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_advocacy#Decolonizing_space), [Space ethics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_ethics), [Ethics of terraforming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethics_of_terraforming), and [Planetary chauvinism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_chauvinism)*

> 
> 
> 
> [Gemini 5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gemini_5) mission badge (1965) connecting spaceflight to colonial endeavours[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Roger_Launiuss_Blog_2011-145)
> 
> ![%E2%AD%90%EF%B8%8F%20SpaceRAG%20Text%201c747df749744da19743ce68743431af/image122.png](%E2%AD%90%EF%B8%8F%20SpaceRAG%20Text%201c747df749744da19743ce68743431af/image122.png)
> 
> The logo and name of the [Lunar Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway) references the [St. Louis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Louis) [Gateway Arch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gateway_Arch),[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-146)which some see as associating Mars with the [American frontier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_frontier)and the [*manifest destiny*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manifest_destiny)mentality of [American settler colonialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Settler_colonialism#Settler_colonialism_in_the_United_States).[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-NPR.org_2015-147)
> 

Space colonization has been discussed as [postcolonial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postcolonialism)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Durrani_2019-98) continuation of [imperialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imperialism) and [colonialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonialism),[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-148)[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Haskins-149)[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Drake-150)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-marshall-151) calling for [decolonization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decolonization) instead of colonization.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Bartels_2018h-152)[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-NatGeo_2018-153)Critics argue that the present politico-legal regimes and their philosophic grounding advantage imperialist development of space,[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-marshall-151)that key decisionmakers in space colonization are often wealthy elites affiliated with private corporations, and that space colonization would primarily appeal to their peers rather than ordinary citizens.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-DNLee-154)[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-155) Furthermore, it is argued that there is a need for inclusive[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-156) and democratic participation and implementation of any space exploration, infrastructure or habitation.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Tavares_Buckner_Burton_McKaig_2020-157)[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-158)According to space law expert Michael Dodge, existing [space law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_law), such as the [Outer Space Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty), guarantees access to space, but does not enforce social inclusiveness or regulate non-state actors.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonization#cite_note-Bartels_2018h-152)
