### **Classification terminology**

The Habitable Exoplanets Catalog[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-PHL-84) uses estimated surface temperature range to classify exoplanets:

- **hypopsychroplanets** - very cold (<−50 °C)
- **psychroplanets** - cold (<−50 to 0 °C)
- **mesoplanets** - medium temperature (0–50 °C; not to be confused with the other definition of [mesoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesoplanet))
- **thermoplanets** - hot (50-100 °C)
- **hyperthermoplanets** - (> 100 °C)

Mesoplanets would be ideal for complex life, whereas hypopsychroplanets and hyperthermoplanets might only support [extremophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extremophile) life.

The HEC uses the following terms to classify exoplanets in terms of mass, from least to greatest: asteroidan, mercurian, subterran, terran, superterran, neptunian, and jovian.

## Alternative star systems

In determining the feasibility of extraterrestrial life, astronomers had long focused their attention on stars like the Sun. However, since planetary systems that resemble the Solar System are proving to be rare, they have begun to explore the possibility that life might form in systems very unlike the Sun's.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-85)[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-86)

It is believed that [F](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/F-type_main-sequence_star), [G](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star), [K](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-type_main-sequence_star) and [M](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M-type_star)-type stars could host habitable exoplanets.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-87) About half of the stars similar in temperature to the Sun could have a rocky planet able to support liquid water on its surface, according to research using data from NASA's [Kepler Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler_Space_Telescope).[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-88)

### **Binary systems**

*Main article: [Habitability of binary star systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitability_of_binary_star_systems)*

Typical estimates often suggest that 50% or more of all stellar systems are [binary systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_star). This may be partly sample bias, as massive and bright stars tend to be in binaries and these are most easily observed and catalogued; a more precise analysis has suggested that the more common fainter stars are usually singular, and that up to two thirds of all stellar systems are therefore solitary.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-89)

The separation between stars in a binary may range from less than one [astronomical unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit) (AU, the average Earth–Sun distance) to several hundred. In latter instances, the gravitational effects will be negligible on a planet orbiting an otherwise suitable star and habitability potential will not be disrupted unless the orbit is highly eccentric (see [Nemesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nemesis_(hypothetical_star)), for example). However, where the separation is significantly less, a stable orbit may be impossible. If a planet's distance to its primary exceeds about one fifth of the closest approach of the other star, orbital stability is not guaranteed.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-90) Whether planets might form in binaries at all had long been unclear, given that gravitational forces might interfere with planet formation. Theoretical work by [Alan Boss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alan_Boss) at the [Carnegie Institution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnegie_Institution) has shown that gas giants can form around stars in binary systems much as they do around solitary stars.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-91)

One study of [Alpha Centauri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_Centauri), the nearest star system to the Sun, suggested that binaries need not be discounted in the search for habitable planets. Centauri A and B have an 11 AU distance at closest approach (23 AU mean), and both should have stable habitable zones. A study of long-term orbital stability for simulated planets within the system shows that planets within approximately three AU of either star may remain rather stable (i.e. the [semi-major axis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semi-major_axis)deviating by less than 5% during 32 000 binary periods). The continuous habitable zone (CHZ for 4.5 billion years) for [Centauri A](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_Centauri) is conservatively estimated at 1.2 to 1.3 AU and Centauri B at 0.73 to 0.74—well within the stable region in both cases.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-92)

### **Red dwarf systems**

*Main article: [Habitability of red dwarf systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitability_of_red_dwarf_systems)*

> 
> 
> 
> Relative star sizes and [photospheric temperatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosphere). Any planet around a red dwarf such as the one shown here ([Gliese 229A](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliese_229A)) would have to huddle close to achieve Earth-like temperatures, probably inducing tidal locking. See [Aurelia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurelia_and_Blue_Moon). Credit: MPIA/V. Joergens.
> 

M-type stars also considered possible hosts of habitable exoplanets, even those with flares such as Proxima b. Determining the habitability of [red dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_dwarf)stars could help determine how common life in the universe might be, as red dwarfs make up between 70 and 90% of all the stars in the galaxy. However, it is important to bear in mind that flare stars could greatly reduce the habitability of exoplanets by eroding their atmosphere.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-93)

### **Size**

Astronomers for many years ruled out red dwarfs as potential abodes for life. Their small size (from 0.08 to 0.45 solar masses) means that their [nuclear reactions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_reaction) proceed exceptionally slowly, and they emit very little light (from 3% of that produced by the Sun to as little as 0.01%). Any planet in orbit around a red dwarf would have to huddle very close to its parent star to attain Earth-like surface temperatures; from 0.3 AU (just inside the orbit of [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet))) for a star like [Lacaille 8760](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lacaille_8760), to as little as 0.032 AU for a star like [Proxima Centauri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxima_Centauri)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-94) (such a world would have a year lasting just 6.3 days). At those distances, the star's gravity would cause tidal locking. One side of the planet would eternally face the star, while the other would always face away from it. The only ways in which potential life could avoid either an inferno or a deep freeze would be if the planet had an atmosphere thick enough to transfer the star's heat from the day side to the night side, or if there was a gas giant in the habitable zone, with a [habitable moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitable_moon), which would be locked to the planet instead of the star, allowing a more even distribution of radiation over the moon. It was long assumed that such a thick atmosphere would prevent sunlight from reaching the surface in the first place, preventing [photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis).

![%E2%AD%90%EF%B8%8F%20SpaceRAG%20Text%201c747df749744da19743ce68743431af/image108.jpg](%E2%AD%90%EF%B8%8F%20SpaceRAG%20Text%201c747df749744da19743ce68743431af/image108.jpg)

An artist's impression of [GJ 667 Cc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GJ_667_Cc), a potentially habitable planet orbiting a red dwarf constituent in a [trinary star system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_system)

This pessimism has been tempered by research. Studies by Robert Haberle and Manoj Joshi of [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA)'s [Ames Research Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ames_Research_Center) in California have shown that a planet's atmosphere (assuming it included greenhouse gases [CO2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide) and [H2O](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_(molecule))) need only be 100 millibars (0.10 atm), for the star's heat to be effectively carried to the night side.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-95) This is well within the levels required for photosynthesis, though water would still remain frozen on the dark side in some of their models. Martin Heath of [Greenwich Community College](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenwich_Community_College), has shown that seawater, too, could be effectively circulated without freezing solid if the ocean basins were deep enough to allow free flow beneath the night side's ice cap. Further research—including a consideration of the amount of photosynthetically active radiation—suggested that tidally locked planets in red dwarf systems might at least be habitable for higher plants.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-96)

### **Other factors limiting habitability**

Size is not the only factor in making red dwarfs potentially unsuitable for life, however. On a red dwarf planet, photosynthesis on the night side would be impossible, since it would never see the sun. On the day side, because the sun does not rise or set, areas in the shadows of mountains would remain so forever. [Photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis) as we understand it would be complicated by the fact that a red dwarf produces most of its radiation in the [infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared), and on the Earth the process depends on visible light. There are potential positives to this scenario. Numerous terrestrial ecosystems rely on [chemosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemosynthesis) rather than photosynthesis, for instance, which would be possible in a red dwarf system. A static primary star position removes the need for plants to steer leaves toward the sun, deal with changing shade/sun patterns, or change from photosynthesis to stored energy during night. Because of the lack of a day-night cycle, including the weak light of morning and evening, far more energy would be available at a given radiation level.

Red dwarfs are far more variable and violent than their more stable, larger cousins. Often they are covered in [starspots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starspot) that can dim their emitted light by up to 40% for months at a time, while at other times they emit gigantic flares that can double their brightness in a matter of minutes.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-97) Such variation would be very damaging for life, as it would not only destroy any complex organic molecules that could possibly form biological precursors, but also because it would blow off sizeable portions of the planet's atmosphere.

For a planet around a red dwarf star to support life, it would require a rapidly rotating magnetic field to protect it from the flares. A tidally locked planet rotates only very slowly, and so cannot produce a geodynamo at its core. The violent flaring period of a red dwarf's life cycle is estimated to only last roughly the first 1.2 billion years of its existence. If a planet forms far away from a red dwarf so as to avoid tidal locking, and then migrates into the star's habitable zone after this turbulent initial period, it is possible that life may have a chance to develop.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-98)However, observations of the 7 to 12-billion year old [Barnard's Star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barnard%27s_Star) showcase that even old red dwarfs can have significant flare activity. Barnard's Star was long assumed to have little activity, but in 1998 astronomers observed an intense [stellar flare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_flare), showing that it is a [flare star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flare_star).[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-Flare-99)

### **Longevity and ubiquity**

Red dwarfs have one advantage over other stars as abodes for life: far greater longevity. It took 4.5 billion years before humanity appeared on Earth, and life as we know it will see suitable conditions for 1[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-100) to 2.3[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-pnas1_24_9576-101) billion years more. Red dwarfs, by contrast, could live for trillions of years because their nuclear reactions are far slower than those of larger stars, meaning that life would have longer to evolve and survive.

While the likelihood of finding a planet in the habitable zone around any specific red dwarf is slight, the total amount of habitable zone around all red dwarfs combined is equal to the total amount around Sun-like stars given their ubiquity.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-102) Furthermore, this total amount of habitable zone will last longer, because red dwarf stars live for hundreds of billions of years or even longer on the main sequence.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-Red_Dwarfs_Universe_Today-103) However, combined with the above disadvantages, it is more likely that red dwarf stars would remain habitable longer to microbes, while the shorter-lived yellow dwarf stars, like the Sun, would remain habitable longer to animals.

### **F-type star systems**

*Main article: [Habitability of F-type main-sequence star systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitability_of_F-type_main-sequence_star_systems)*

### **G-type star systems**

*Main article: [Habitability of yellow dwarf systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitability_of_yellow_dwarf_systems)*

G-type stars would allow to host the exoplanets most similar to Earth, that is, [Earth-like planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_analog).[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-104)

### **K-type star systems**

*Main article: [Habitability of K-type main-sequence star systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitability_of_K-type_main-sequence_star_systems)*

K-type stars would provide the necessary conditions for [super habitable exoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superhabitable_planet), which are exoplanets that could be more habitable than Earth.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-105)

### **Massive stars**

Recent research suggests that very large stars, greater than ~100 solar masses, could have planetary systems consisting of hundreds of Mercury-sized planets within the habitable zone. Such systems could also contain [brown dwarfs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_dwarfs) and low-mass stars (~0.1–0.3 solar masses).[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-106) However the very short lifespans of stars of more than a few solar masses would scarcely allow time for a planet to cool, let alone the time needed for a stable biosphere to develop. Massive stars are thus eliminated as possible abodes for life.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-107)

However, a massive-star system could be a progenitor of life in another way – the [supernova](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova)explosion of the massive star in the central part of the system. This supernova will disperse heavier elements throughout its vicinity, created during the phase when the massive star has moved off of the main sequence, and the systems of the potential low-mass stars (which are still on the main sequence) within the former massive-star system may be enriched with the relatively large supply of the heavy elements so close to a supernova explosion. However, this states nothing about what types of planets would form as a result of the supernova material, or what their habitability potential would be.

### **Neutron stars**

*Main article: [Habitability of neutron star systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitability_of_neutron_star_systems)*

### **Post-main sequence stars**

*Main article: [Red giant § Prospects for habitability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant#Prospects_for_habitability)*

## Four classes of habitable planets based on water

In a review of the factors which are important for the evolution of habitable Earth-sized planets, Lammer et al. proposed a classification of four water-dependent habitat types:[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-Review_2009-22)[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-Forget_2013-108)

**Class I** habitats are planetary bodies on which stellar and geophysical conditions allow liquid water to be available at the surface, along with sunlight, so that complex [multicellular organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multicellular_organism) may originate.

**Class II** habitats include bodies which initially enjoy Earth-like conditions, but do not keep their ability to sustain liquid water on their surface due to stellar or geophysical conditions. Mars, and possibly Venus are examples of this class where complex life forms may not develop.

**Class III** habitats are planetary bodies where liquid water oceans exist below the surface, where they can interact directly with a silicate-rich [core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_core).

> Such a situation can be expected on water-rich planets located too far from their star to allow surface liquid water, but on which subsurface water is in liquid form because of the geothermal heat. Two examples of such an environment are Europa and Enceladus. In such worlds, not only is light not available as an energy source, but the organic material brought by meteorites (thought to have been necessary to start life in some scenarios) may not easily reach the liquid water. If a planet can only harbor life below its surface, the biosphere would not likely modify the whole planetary environment in an observable way, thus, detecting its presence on an exoplanet would be extremely difficult.
> 

**Class IV** habitats have liquid water layers between two ice layers, or liquids above ice.

> If the water layer is thick enough, water at its base will be in solid phase (ice polymorphs) because of the high pressure. Ganymede and Callisto are likely examples of this class. Their oceans are thought to be enclosed between thick ice layers. In such conditions, the emergence of even simple life forms may be very difficult because the necessary ingredients for life will likely be completely diluted.
> 

## The galactic neighborhood

Along with the characteristics of planets and their star systems, the wider galactic environment may also impact habitability. Scientists considered the possibility that particular areas of galaxies ([galactic habitable zones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_habitable_zone)) are better suited to life than others; the Solar System, in the [Orion Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_Arm), on the Milky Way galaxy's edge is considered to be in a life-favorable spot:[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-109)

- It is not in a [globular cluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globular_cluster) where immense star densities are inimical to life, given excessive radiation and gravitational disturbance. Globular clusters are also primarily composed of older, probably metal-poor, stars. Furthermore, in globular clusters, the great ages of the stars would mean a large amount of [stellar evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_evolution) by the host or other nearby stars, which due to their proximity may cause extreme harm to life on any planets, provided that they can form.
- It is not near an active [gamma ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma_ray) source.
- It is not near the galactic center where once again star densities increase the likelihood of ionizing radiation (e.g., from [magnetars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetar) and [supernovae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernovae)). The [supermassive black holes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole) at the centers of galaxies may also prove a danger to any nearby bodies.
- The circular orbit of the Sun around the galactic center keeps it out of the way of the galaxy's spiral arms where intense radiation and gravitation may again lead to disruption.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-110)

Thus, relative isolation is ultimately what a life-bearing system needs. If the Sun were crowded amongst other systems, the chance of being fatally close to dangerous radiation sources would increase significantly. Further, close neighbors might disrupt the stability of various orbiting bodies such as [Oort cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oort_cloud) and [Kuiper belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuiper_belt) objects, which can bring catastrophe if knocked into the inner Solar System.

While stellar crowding proves disadvantageous to habitability, so too does extreme isolation. A star as metal-rich as the Sun would probably not have formed in the very outermost regions of the Milky Way given a decline in the relative abundance of metals and a general lack of star formation. Thus, a "suburban" location, such as the Solar System enjoys, is preferable to a Galaxy's center or farthest reaches.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-111)

## Other considerations

### **Alternative biochemistries**

*Main article: [Hypothetical types of biochemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypothetical_types_of_biochemistry)*

While most investigations of extraterrestrial life start with the assumption that advanced life-forms must have similar requirements for life as on Earth, the hypothesis of [other types of biochemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypothetical_types_of_biochemistry) suggests the possibility of lifeforms evolving around a different metabolic mechanism. In [*Evolving the Alien*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolving_the_Alien), [biologist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biologist) [Jack Cohen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jack_Cohen_(scientist)) and [mathematician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematician) [Ian Stewart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ian_Stewart_(mathematician)) argue [astrobiology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrobiology), based on the [Rare Earth hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rare_Earth_hypothesis), is restrictive and unimaginative. They suggest that [Earth-like planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_analog) may be very rare, but non-carbon-based complex life could possibly emerge in other environments. The most frequently mentioned alternative to carbon is [silicon-based life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon-based_life), while [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia-based_life#Ammonia) and [hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrocarbons) are sometimes suggested as [alternative solvents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypothetical_types_of_biochemistry#Non-water_solvents) to water. The astrobiologist [Dirk Schulze-Makuch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirk_Schulze-Makuch) and other scientists have proposed a Planet Habitability Index whose criteria include "potential for holding a liquid solvent" that is not necessarily restricted to water.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-112)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-113)

More speculative ideas have focused on bodies altogether different from Earth-like planets. Astronomer [Frank Drake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Drake), a well-known proponent of the search for [extraterrestrial life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life), imagined life on a [neutron star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_star): submicroscopic "nuclear molecules" combining to form creatures with a life cycle millions of times quicker than Earth life.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-114) Called "imaginative and tongue-in-cheek", the idea gave rise to science fiction depictions.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-115) [Carl Sagan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Sagan), another optimist with regards to extraterrestrial life, considered the possibility of organisms that are always airborne within the high atmosphere of Jupiter in a 1976 paper.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-Sagan,_C._1976-44)[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-Darling-45) Cohen and Stewart also envisioned life in both a solar environment and in the atmosphere of a gas giant.

### **"Good Jupiters"**

"Good Jupiters" are gas giants, like the Solar System's [Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter), that orbit their stars in circular orbits far enough away from the habitable zone not to disturb it but close enough to "protect" terrestrial planets in closer orbit in two critical ways. First, they help to stabilize the orbits, and thereby the climates of the inner planets. Second, they keep the inner stellar system relatively free of comets and asteroids that could cause devastating impacts.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-116) Jupiter orbits the Sun at about five times the distance between the Earth and the Sun. This is the rough distance we should expect to find good Jupiters elsewhere. Jupiter's "caretaker" role was dramatically illustrated in 1994 when [Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9) impacted the giant.[[*according to whom?*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions)]

However, the evidence is not quite so clear. Research has shown that Jupiter's role in determining the rate at which objects hit Earth is significantly more complicated than once thought.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-117)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-118)[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-119)[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-120)

The role of Jupiter in the early history of the Solar System is somewhat better established, and the source of significantly less debate.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-121)[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-122) Early in the Solar System's history, Jupiter is accepted as having played an important role in the hydration of our planet: it increased the eccentricity of [asteroid belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_belt) orbits and enabled many to cross Earth's orbit and supply the planet with important volatiles such as water and carbon dioxide. Before Earth reached half its present mass, icy bodies from the Jupiter–Saturn region and small bodies from the primordial asteroid belt supplied water to the Earth due to the gravitational scattering of Jupiter and, to a lesser extent, [Saturn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saturn).[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-123) Thus, while the gas giants are now helpful protectors, they were once suppliers of critical habitability material.

In contrast, Jupiter-sized bodies that orbit too close to the habitable zone but not in it (as in [47 Ursae Majoris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/47_Ursae_Majoris)), or have a highly elliptical orbit that crosses the habitable zone (like [16 Cygni B](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/16_Cygni)) make it very difficult for an independent Earth-like planet to exist in the system. See the discussion of a [stable habitable zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#A_stable_habitable_zone) above. However, during the process of migrating into a habitable zone, a Jupiter-size planet may capture a terrestrial planet as a moon. Even if such a planet is initially loosely bound and following a strongly inclined orbit, gravitational interactions with the star can stabilize the new moon into a close, circular orbit that is coplanar with the planet's orbit around the star.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-124)

### **Life's impact on habitability**

A supplement to the factors that support life's emergence is the notion that life itself, once formed, becomes a habitability factor in its own right. An important Earth example was the production of molecular oxygen gas (O2) by ancient [cyanobacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanobacteria), and eventually photosynthesizing plants, leading to a radical change in the composition of Earth's atmosphere. This environmental change is called the [Great Oxidation Event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Oxidation_Event). This oxygen proved fundamental to the [respiration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)) of later animal species. The [Gaia hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis), a scientific model of the geo-biosphere pioneered by [James Lovelock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Lovelock) in 1975, argues that life as a whole fosters and maintains suitable conditions for itself by helping to create a planetary environment suitable for its continuity. Similarly, [David Grinspoon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Grinspoon) has suggested a "living worlds hypothesis" in which our understanding of what constitutes habitability cannot be separated from life already extant on a planet. Planets that are geologically and meteorologically alive are much more likely to be biologically alive as well and "a planet and its life will co-evolve."[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-125) This is the basis of [Earth system science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_system_science).

### **The role of chance**

In 2020, a computer simulation of the evolution of planetary climates over 3 billion years suggested that feedbacks are a necessary but not a sufficient condition for preventing planets from ever becoming too hot or cold for life, and that chance also plays a crucial role.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-126)[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-10.1038/s43247-020-00057-8-127)Related considerations include yet unknown factors influencing the thermal habitability of planets such as "feedback mechanism (or mechanisms) that prevents the climate ever wandering to fatal temperatures".[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability#cite_note-128)

The following list include some of the **potentially habitable exoplanets** discovered so far. It is mostly based on estimates of habitability by the Habitable Worlds Catalog (HWC), and data from the [NASA Exoplanet Archive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Exoplanet_Archive). The HWC is maintained by the [Planetary Habitability Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_Habitability_Laboratory) at the [University of Puerto Rico at Arecibo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Puerto_Rico_at_Arecibo).[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1) There is also a speculative list being developed of [superhabitable planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superhabitable_planet).

Surface [planetary habitability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability) is thought to require an orbit at the right distance from the host star for liquid surface water to be present, in addition to various [geophysical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geophysics) and [geodynamical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodynamics) aspects, atmospheric density, radiation type and intensity, and the host star's [plasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plasma_(physics)) environment.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Review_2009-2)

## List

This is a list of [exoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet) within the [circumstellar habitable zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone) that are under 10 Earth masses and smaller than 2.5 Earth radii, and thus have a chance of being [rocky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_planet).[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-M%C3%A9ndez_Gonz%C3%A1lez-Espada_2016_p._1-3)[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1) Note that inclusion on this list does not guarantee habitability, and in particular the larger planets are unlikely to have a rocky composition.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-ChenKipping2017-4) Earth is included for comparison.

Note that mass and radius values prefixed with "~" have not been measured, but are estimated from a mass-radius relationship.

Earth and Venus were included for reference.

## Previous candidates

Some exoplanet candidates detected by [radial velocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doppler_spectroscopy) that were originally thought to be potentially habitable were later found to most likely be artifacts of stellar activity. These include [Gliese 581 d](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliese_581_d) & [g](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliese_581_g),[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Robertson2014-50)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Hatzes2015-51)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Dodson-Robinson2022-52) [Gliese 667](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliese_667) Ce & f,[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-ferozhobson2014-12)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-RobertsonMahadevan2014-53) [Gliese 682](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliese_682) b & c,[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Feng2020-7) [Kapteyn b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kapteyn_b),[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Robertson2015-54)[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Bortle2021-55) and [Gliese 832 c](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliese_832_c).[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Gorrini2022-56)

[HD 85512 b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HD_85512_b) was initially estimated to be potentially habitable,[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Kaltenegger2011-57)[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-old1-58) but updated models for the boundaries of the habitable zone placed the planet interior to the HZ,[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Kopparapu2013-59)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-newHZ-60) and it is now considered non-habitable.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1) [Kepler-69c](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler-69c) has gone through a similar process; though initially estimated to be potentially habitable,[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Barclay2013-61) it was quickly realized that the planet is more likely to be similar to Venus,[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Kane2013-62) and is thus no longer considered habitable.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1) Several other planets, such as [Gliese 180 b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliese_180_b), also appear to be examples of planets once considered potentially habitable but later found to be interior to the habitable zone.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1)

Similarly, [Tau Ceti e](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tau_Ceti_e) and [f](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tau_Ceti_f) were initially both considered potentially habitable,[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-taucet-63) but with improved models of the circumstellar habitable zone, as of 2022 PHL does not consider either planet potentially habitable.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1)[[*failed verification*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability)] [Kepler-438b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler-438b) was also initially considered potentially habitable; however, it was later found to be a subject of powerful [flares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flare_star) that can strip a planet of its atmosphere, so it is now considered non-habitable.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1)

[K2-3d](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K2-3d) and [K2-18b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K2-18b) were originally considered potentially habitable, and the latter remains listed in the HEC,[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-HEC-1) but recent studies have shown them to be gaseous [sub-Neptunes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mini-Neptune) and thus unlikely to be habitable.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-64)[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-65)[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-66)[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-Damasso2018-67)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-68)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-69)

KOI-1686.01 was also considered a potentially habitable exoplanet after its detection in 2011, until proven a false positive by [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA) in 2015.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_potentially_habitable_exoplanets#cite_note-70) Several other KOIs, like [Kepler-577b](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kepler-577b&action=edit&redlink=1) and [Kepler-1649b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler-1649b), were considered potentially habitable prior to confirmation, but with new data are no longer considered habitable.
